
Preface
When teaching a graduate topology course, itâ€™s tempting to rush through the point-set
topology, or even skip it altogether, and do more algebraic topology, which is more fun
to teach and more relevant to todayâ€™s students. Many point-set topology ideas are already
familiar to students from real analysis or undergraduate point-set topology courses and
may seem safe to skip. Also, point-set ideas that might be unfamiliar but important in
other subjects, say the Zariski topology in algebraic geometry or the p-adic topology in
number theory, can be picked up later when they are encountered in context.
An alternative to rushing through point-set topology is to cover it from a more modern,
categorical point of view. We think this alternative is better for several reasons. Since
many students are familiar with point-set ideas already, they are in a good position to learn
something new about these ideas, like the universal properties characterizing them. Plus,
using categorical methods to handle point-set topology, whose name even suggests an old-
fashioned way of thinking of spaces, demonstrates the power and versatility of the methods.
The category of topological spaces is poorly behaved in some respects, but this provides
opportunities to draw meaningful contrasts between topology and other subjects and to
give good reasons why some kinds of spaces (like compactly generated weakly Hausdorff
spaces) enjoy particular prevalence. Finally, there is the practicality that point-set topology
is on the syllabus for our ï¬rst-year topology courses and PhD exams. Teaching the material
in a way that both deepens understanding and prepares a solid foundation for future work
in modern mathematics is an excellent alternative.
This text contains material curated from many resources to present elementary topology
from a categorical perspective. In particular, we cover some of the same topics as Ronnie
Brown (2006), although our outlook is, from the outset, more categorical. The result is
intentionally less comprehensive but more widely useful. We assume that students know
linear algebra well and have had at least enough abstract algebra to understand how to
form the quotient of a group by a normal subgroup. Students should also have some basic
knowledge about how to work with sets and their elements, even as they endeavor to work
with arrows instead. Students encountering diagrams and arrows for the ï¬rst time may

x
Preface
want to spend a little extra time reading the preliminaries where the objects (sets) are
presumably familiar but the perspective may be new.
Covering spaces, homology, and cohomology are not in this book, but students will be
ready to learn more algebraic topology after reading through our text. The omitted top-
ics are likely included in whichever algebraic topology book is used afterward, including
Massey (1991), Rotman (1998), May (1999), Hatcher (2002), and tom Dieck (2008), for
which the reader will be well prepared. When we teach the ï¬rst semester topology course
in our PhD program, we usually cover the classiï¬cation of compact surfaces. While this
classiï¬cation theorem is not in the text, an instructor may wish to cover it in their course,
and it is hard to beat Conwayâ€™s ZIP proof or the proof in Massey (1991).
With detailed descriptions of topological constructions emphasizing universal proper-
ties; ï¬lter-based treatment of convergence; thorough discussions of limits, colimits, and
adjunctions; and an early emphasis on homotopy, this book guides the student of topology
through the important transition from an undergraduate with a solid background in analysis
or point-set topology to a graduate student preparing to work on problems in contemporary
mathematics.

Introduction.
Traditionally, the ï¬rst chapter of a textbook on mathematics begins by recalling
basic notions from set theory. This chapter begins by introducing basic notions from category the-
ory, the shift being from the internal anatomy of sets to their relationships with other sets. The
idea of focusing on the relationships between mathematical objects, rather than on their internals,
is fundamental to modern mathematics, and category theory is the framework for working from
this perspective. Our goal for chapter 0 is to present what is perhaps familiar to youâ€”functions,
sets, topological spacesâ€”from the contemporary perspective of category theory. Notably, category
theory originated in topology in the 1940s with work of Samuel Eilenberg and Saunders MacLane
(Eilenberg and MacLane, 1945).
This chapterâ€™s material is organized into three sections. Section 0.1 begins with a quick review of
topological spaces, bases, and continuous functions. Motivated by a few key features of topological
spaces and continuous functions, weâ€™ll proceed to section 0.2 and introduce three basic concepts of
category theory: categories, functors, and natural transformations. The same section highlights one
of the main philosophies of category theory, namely that studying a mathematical object is akin to
studying its relationships to other objects. This golden thread starts in section 0.2 and weaves its way
through the remaining pages of the bookâ€”we encourage you to keep an eye out for the occasional
glimmer. Finally, equipped with the categorical mindset, weâ€™ll revisit some familiar ideas from basic
set theory in section 0.3.
0.1
Basic Topology
Deï¬nition 0.1 A topological space (X, T ) consists of a set X and a collection T of subsets
of X that satisfy the following properties:
(i)
The empty set âˆ…and X are in T .
(ii)
Any union of elements in T is also in T .
(iii)
Any ï¬nite intersection of elements in T is also in T .

2
Chapter 0
The collection T is called a topology on X, and weâ€™ll write X in place of (X, T ) if the
topology is understood. Occasionally, weâ€™ll also refer to the topological space X as simply
a space. Elements of the topology T are called open sets, and a set is called closed if and
only if its complement is open.
Example 0.1 Suppose X is any set. The collection 2X of all subsets of X forms a topology
called the discrete topology on X, and the set {âˆ…, X} forms a topology on X called the
indiscrete topology or the trivial topology.
Sometimes, two topologies on the same set are comparable. When T âŠ†T â€², the topology
T can be called coarser than T â€², or the topology T â€² can be called ï¬ner than T . Instead of
coarser and ï¬ner, some people say â€œsmaller and largerâ€ or â€œweaker and stronger,â€ but the
terminology becomes clearerâ€”as with most things in lifeâ€”with coffee. A coarse grind
yields a small number of chunky coffee pieces, whereas a ï¬ne grind results in a large
number of tiny coffee pieces. Finely ground beans make stronger coffee; coarsely ground
beans make weaker coffee.
In practice, it can be easier to work with a small collection of open subsets of X that
generates the topology.
Deï¬nition 0.2 A collection B of subsets of a set X is a basis for a topology on X if and
only if
(i)
For each x âˆˆX there is a B âˆˆB such that x âˆˆB.
(ii)
If x âˆˆAâˆ©B where A, B âˆˆB, then there is at least one C âˆˆB such that x âˆˆC âŠ†Aâˆ©B.
The topology T generated by the basis B is deï¬ned to be the coarsest topology containing
B. Equivalently, a set U âŠ†X is open in the topology generated by the basis B if and only
if for every x âˆˆU there is a B âˆˆB such that x âˆˆB âŠ†U.
We call the collection of B âˆˆB with x âˆˆB the basic open neighborhoods of x. More
generally, in any topology T , those U âˆˆT containing x are called open neighborhoods of
x and together are denoted Tx.
Example 0.2 A metric space is a pair (X, d) where X is a set and d: X Ã— X â†’R is a
function satisfying
â€¢ d(x, y) â‰¥0 for all x, y âˆˆX,
â€¢ d(x, y) = d(y, x) for all x, y âˆˆX,
â€¢ d(x, y) + d(y, z) â‰¥d(x, z) for all x, y, z âˆˆX,
â€¢ d(x, y) = 0 if and only if x = y for all x, y âˆˆX.
The function d is called a metric or a distance function. If (X, d) is a metric space, x âˆˆX,
and r > 0, then the ball centered at x of radius r is deï¬ned to be
B(x, r) = {y âˆˆX | d(x, y) < r} .

3
The balls {B(x, r)} form a basis for a topology on X called the metric topology. So any
set with a metric gives rise to a topological space. On the other hand, if Y is a space with
topology T and if there is a metric d on Y such that the metric topology is the same as T ,
then Y is said to be metrizable.
Any subset of a metric space is a metric space. In particular, subsets of Rn provide numer-
ous examples of topological spaces since Rn with the usual Euclidean distance function is
a metric space. For example,
â€¢ the real line R,
â€¢ the unit interval I := [0, 1],

Weâ€™ll see more examples of topological spaces in chapter 1 and will discuss some im-
portant features in chapter 2. One way weâ€™ll glean information is by studying how spaces
relate to each other. These relationships are best understood as functions that interact
nicelyâ€”in the sense of the following deï¬nitionâ€”with open subsets of the spaces.
Deï¬nition 0.3 A function f : X â†’Y between two topological spaces is continuous if and
only if f âˆ’1U is open in X whenever U is open in Y.
It is straightforward to check that for any topological space X, the identity idX : X â†’X is
continuous, and for any topological spaces X, Y, Z and any continuous functions f : X â†’Y
and g: Y â†’Z, the composition g f := g â—¦f : X â†’Z is continuous, and moreover that
this composition is associative. On the surface, these observations may appear to be ho-
hum, but quite the opposite is true. Collectively, the seemingly routine observations above
amount to the statement that topological spaces together with continuous functions form a
category.

â€¢ Grp: The objects are groups, the morphisms are group homomorphisms, and compo-

Category theory is an appropriate setting in which to discuss an age-old question: â€œWhen
are two objects really the same?â€ The concept of â€œsamenessâ€ being a special kind of re-
lationship means that objects are the same if there is a particular morphismâ€”an isomor-
phismâ€”between them. How we talk about sameness is at the heart of category theory. We
take Wittgensteinâ€™s (1922) critique,
Roughly speaking, to say of two things that they are identical is nonsense, and to say of
one thing that it is identical with itself is to say nothing at all
as an invitation to ask for less, namely isomorphic (or uniquely isomorphic) objects, rather
than identical objects.
Deï¬nition 0.5 Let X and Y be objects in any category, and suppose f : X â†’Y.
(i)
f is left invertible if and only if there exists a morphism g: Y â†’X so that g f = idX.
The morphism g is called a left inverse of f.
(ii)
f is right invertible if and only if there exists a morphism h: Y â†’X so that fh = idY.
The morphism h is called a right inverse of f.
In the case when f has both a left inverse g and a right inverse h, then
g = g idY = g fh = idX h = h
and the single morphism g = h is called the inverse of f. (We encourage you to verify our
use of â€œthe.â€ If f has an inverse, it is unique.) Therefore

(iii)
f is invertible and is said to be an isomorphism if it is both left and right invertible.
Two objects X and Y are isomorphic, denoted X  Y, if there exists an isomorphism

The notion of being isomorphic is a form of equivalence, meaning it is reï¬‚exive, sym-
metric, and transitiveâ€”properties you should check. Isomorphic objects, therefore, form
equivalence classes. Some categories have their own special terminology for both isomor-
phism and these isomorphism classes. For instance,
â€¢ Isomorphisms in Set are called bijections, and two isomorphic sets are said to have
the same cardinality. A cardinal is an isomorphism class of sets.
â€¢ Isomorphisms in Top are called homeomorphisms, and two isomorphic spaces are said
to be homeomorphic.
â€¢ Isomorphisms in hTop are called homotopy equivalences, and isomorphic spaces in
hTop are said to be homotopic. (See section 1.6 for a discussion of homotopy.)
Mathematics is particularly concerned with properties that are preserved under isomor-
phisms in a given category. For instance, topology is essentially the study of properties
that are preserved under homeomorphisms. Such properties are referred to as topological
properties and distinguish spaces: if X and Y are homeomorphic and X has (or doesnâ€™t
have) a certain property, then Y must have (or cannot have) that property, too.
Example 0.4 The cardinality of a topological space is a topological property since any
homeomorphism f : X â†’Y is necessarily a bijective function, and so X and Y, when
viewed as sets, must have the same cardinality. Metrizability is also a topological prop-
erty. Connectedness (section 2.1), compactness (section 2.3), Hausdorff (section 2.2), ï¬rst
countability (section 3.2), . . . are examples of other topological properties that will be dis-
cussed in the indicated sections.
However, not every familiar property is a topological one.
Example 0.5 A metric space is called complete if every Cauchy sequence converges. Be-
ing a complete metric space is not a topological property. For instance, the map (âˆ’1, 1) â†’

This example also shows that being bounded is also not a topological property: a metric
space is said to be bounded if the metric is a bounded function. Clearly, (âˆ’1, 1) is bounded
while R is not.
Comparing an object X with another object can help us to understand X better. Taking this
idea further, we may also compare X with all objects at once. In other words, we can learn
a great deal of information about X by looking at all morphisms both out of and in to X.
This is the content of the next theorem. In essence, it states that the isomorphism class of
an object is completely determined by morphisms to and from it. This is one of the main
maxims of category theory:

In summary, if you understand all the morphisms X â†’Z, then you know X up to iso-

Now it turns out that categories themselves are objects worthy of study. And as per our
maxim above, studying a category should amount to studying its relationships to other
categories. But what is a relationship between categories? It is called a functor. That is the
subject of the next section.
You might wonder if we are about to construct a category of categories where the objects
are categories and the morphisms are functors. This can be done, but there are a few impor-
tant considerations. One consideration is size. When we deï¬ned categories, we assumed
that for any two objects X and Y, there is a set of morphisms C(X, Y). This is convenient
so that, as in the case of the pushforward fâˆ—: C(Z, X) â†’C(Z, Y) of a morphism f : X â†’Y
in the previous theorem, we can consider functions between sets of morphisms. However,
if C and D are categories, then there may be more than a setâ€™s worth of functors C â†’D.
Thereâ€™s also another, more subtle consideration to think about: The sharpest identiï¬cation
of an object generally available when doing category is up to unique isomorphism. This
should apply to the morphisms between categories as well. It can be dizzying, but the
takeaway is that invertible functors provide a notion of equivalence thatâ€™s too rigid to be
useful. So instead of an â€œisomorphism of categories,â€ we talk about an â€œequivalence of
categories,â€ which is deï¬ned in a more subtle way. A third consideration is that one might
want to remember that categories themselves have objects and morphisms, and the cate-
gory of categories has more structure than a categoryâ€”itâ€™s probably better to think of it as
a â€œhigherâ€ category, a concept we wonâ€™t stop to discuss here.
0.2.2
Functors
Deï¬nition 0.7 A functor F from a category C to a category D consists of the following
data:
(i)
An object FX of the category D for each object X in the category C,
(ii)
A morphism F f : FX â†’FY for every morphism f : X â†’Y.
These data must be compatible with composition and identity morphisms in the following
sense:
(iii)
(Fg)(F f) = F(g f) for any morphisms f : X â†’Y and g: Y â†’Z,
(iv)
F idX = idFX for any object X.
A functor as deï¬ned above is sometimes described as a covariant functor to distinguish it
from its contravariant counterpart. A functor F : Cop â†’D whose domain is the opposite
category is called a contravariant functor from C to D. Contravariant functors â€œreverse

arrows.â€ That is, for every morphism f : X â†’Y, the functor F : Cop â†’D assigns a
morphism F f : FY â†’FX. We sometimes abuse terminology and refer to a contravariant
functor simply as a functor. This usually doesnâ€™t cause confusion since the direction of the
arrows can often be worked out easily.

A homology theory, for example, is a functor H : Top â†’RMod and is an excellent means
of distinguishing topological spaces: if HX and HY are not isomorphic R-modules, then X

Having adopted the perspective that functors are invariants, itâ€™s natural to wonder: â€œWhen

with all other objects. This theme ï¬nds its origins in anâ€”if not the mostâ€”important result
in category theory.

Example 0.7 Two sets X and Y are isomorphic if and only if Set(Z, X)  Set(Z, Y) for

0.3
Basic Set Theory
Weâ€™ll begin with a brief review of functions.
0.3.1
Functions
A function is said to be injective if and only if it is left cancellative. That is, f : X â†’Y
is injective if and only if for all functions g1, g2 : Z â†’X with fg1 = fg2, it follows that
g1 = g2. Equivalently, f is injective if and only if fâˆ—: Set(Z, X) â†’Set(Z, Y) is injective
for all Z. Yet another equivalent deï¬nition is that f is injective if and only if it has a left
inverse, that is, if and only if there exists g: Y â†’X so that g f = idY. Note that the
composition of injective functions is injective; also, for any f : X â†’Y and g: Y â†’Z, if
g f is injective, then f is injective. We will denote injective functions by hooked arrows, as
in f : X ,â†’Y.
More generally, left-cancellative morphisms in any category are called monomorphisms
or said to be monic and are denoted with arrows with tails as in X â†£Y. In this more
general case, left invertible implies left cancellative, but not conversely. For example, the
map n 7â†’2n deï¬nes a left-cancellative group homomorphism f : Z/2Z â†’Z/4Z. However,
there is no group homomorphism g: Z/4Z â†’Z/2Z so that g f = idZ/2Z .
A function is said to be surjective if and only if it is right cancellative. That is, f : X â†’Y
is surjective if and only if for all functions g1, g2 : Y â†’Z with g1 f = g2 f, it follows that
g1 = g2. Equivalently, f is surjective if and only if f âˆ—: Set(Y, Z) â†’Set(X, Z) is injective
for all Z or, equivalent still, if and only if it has a right inverse. That is, f : X â†’Y is
surjective if and only if there exists g: Y â†’X so that fg = idX. The composition of
surjective functions is surjective, and for any f : X â†’Y and g: Y â†’Z, if g f is surjective,
then g is surjective. Surjective functions will be denoted with two headed arrows as in
f : X â† Y.
In general, right-cancellative morphisms in any category are called epimorphisms or said
to be epic and are also denoted with two-headed arrows. Right invertible implies right

15
cancellative in any category, but not conversely. (You are asked to provide an example in
exercise 0.3 at the end of the chapter.)
Finally, note that in Set a function that is both injective and surjective is an isomor-
phism. This is because left invertible and right invertible imply invertible. (You should
check that having a left inverse and a right inverse both imply thereâ€™s a single two-sided
inverse). But left cancellative and right cancellative together do not imply invertible: there
are categoriesâ€”and Top is one of themâ€”that have morphisms that are both monic and
epic, which nonetheless fail to be isomorphisms.

The empty set âˆ…is initial in Set. That is, for any set X there is a unique function âˆ…â†’X.
On the other hand, the one-point set âˆ—is terminal. That is, for any set X, there is a unique
function X â†’âˆ—. You might take issue with the deï¬nite article â€œtheâ€ in â€œthe one-point set,â€
but it is standard to use the deï¬nite article in circumstances that are unique up to unique
isomorphism. That is the case here: if âˆ—and âˆ—â€² are both one-point sets, then there is a
unique isomorphism âˆ—
âˆ’â†’âˆ—â€². Note also that this terminology is not unique to Set: we can
make sense of initial and terminal objects in any category, though such objects may not
always exist. An object C in a category C is called terminal if for every object X in C there
is a unique morphism X â†’C. Dually, an object D is called initial if for every object X in

0.3.4
Products and Coproducts in Any Category
The universal properties outlined above provide a template for deï¬ning products and co-
productsâ€”and more generally limits and colimitsâ€”in any category. A more complete
discussion is found in chapter 4, but for now, weâ€™d like to point out that in an arbitrary
category, products and coproducts may not exist, and when they do they might not look
like disjoint unions or Cartesian products. For example, the category Fld of ï¬elds doesnâ€™t
have products: if there were a ï¬eld k that were the product of F2 and F3, then there would
be homomorphisms k â†’F2 and k â†’F3. But this is impossible since the characteristic
of k would be equal to both 2 and 3. On the other hand the category Vectk and more
generally RMod has both products and coproducts. Products are Cartesian products, but
coproducts are direct sums. In Grp coproducts are free products, while in the category of
abelian groups, coproducts are direct sums. Even in the category Set, there is something
to say about the existence of products and coproducts. The axiom of choice is precisely the
statement that for any nonempty collection of sets {XÎ±}Î±âˆˆA, the product Q XÎ± exists and is
nonempty. (For more on the axiom of choice, see section 3.4.)
Even though products and coproducts in an arbitrary category might look different than
they do in Set, the constructions are closely related to the constructions in Set. Thatâ€™s
because the universal properties of products and coproducts in an arbitrary category C
yield bijections of sets

0.3.6
Partially Ordered Sets
A partially ordered set or poset is a set P together with a relation â‰¤on P that is reï¬‚exive,
transitive, and antisymmetric. Reï¬‚exive means that for all a âˆˆP, a â‰¤a; transitive means
that for all a, b, c âˆˆP, if a â‰¤b and b â‰¤c, then a â‰¤c; antisymmetric means that for all
a, b âˆˆP, if a â‰¤b and b â‰¤a, then a = b.
One can view a poset as a category whose objects are the elements of P by declaring
there to be a morphism a â†’b if and only if a â‰¤b. Transitivity says not only can composi-
tion be deï¬ned, but it may only be deï¬ned in one way since thereâ€™s at most one morphism
between objects. Alternatively, one can deï¬ne a poset to be a (small) category with the
property that thereâ€™s at most one morphism between objects.
We expect that you have encountered different deï¬nitions for some of the concepts in
this section than what we have provided. In your previous work, an injective function,
for example, might have been deï¬ned in terms of what it does to the elements of the
domain. Here, instead, weâ€™ve deï¬ned an injective function in terms of how it interacts
with other functions. For another example, instead of deï¬ning what the disjoint X ` Y is
(which ultimately involves the Zermelo-Fraenkel axioms of union and extension), weâ€™ve
characterized it up to isomorphism by explaining how it interacts with other sets, bringing
back to mind the Lawvere quote that opened the chapter.

Introduction.
Our goal in this chapter is to construct new topological spaces from given ones.
Weâ€™ll do so by focusing on four basic constructions: subspaces in section 1.2, quotients in section
1.3, products in section 1.4, and coproducts in section 1.5. To maintain a categorical perspective, the
discussion of each construction will ï¬t into the following template:
â€¢ The classic deï¬nition: an explicit construction of the topological space
â€¢ The ï¬rst characterization: a description of the topology as either the coarsest or the ï¬nest
topology for which maps into or out of the space are continuous, leading to a better deï¬nition
â€¢ The second characterization: a description of the topology in terms of a universal property as
given in theorems 1.1, 1.2, 1.3, and 1.4
Before we construct topological spaces, it will be good to have some examples in mind. Weâ€™ll begin
then in section 1.1 with examples of topological spaces and continuous maps between them.
1.1

Letâ€™s open with examples of spaces followed by examples of continuous functions.

1.2
The Subspace Topology
Given a set X, we can obtain a new set by choosing a subset Y of X. If X is endowed with
a topology, weâ€™d like a way to see Y as a topological space, too. This leads to the ï¬rst of
the four constructionsâ€”a subspace. The subspace topology is often deï¬ned (for example,
in Munkres (2000)) as follows:
Deï¬nition 1.1 Let (X, TX) be a topological space and let Y be any subset of X. The sub-
space topology on Y is given by TY := {U âˆ©Y | U âˆˆTX}.
You can and are encouraged to check that this deï¬nition does indeed deï¬ne a topology on
Y. Whatâ€™s more interesting, though, is the property it satisï¬es. In particular, Y naturally
comes with an inclusion map i: Y â†’X, and the subspace topology is the coarsest topology
on Y for which i is continuous. This is its ï¬rst characterization.
1.2.1
The First Characterization
Before we elaborate on this characterization, letâ€™s consider a more general situation by way
of motivation. Let (X, TX) be a topological space and let S be any set whatsoever. Consider
a function
f : S â†’X
It makes no sense to ask if f is continuous until S is equipped with a topology. There
always exist topologies on the set S that will make f continuousâ€”the discrete topology is
one. But is there a coarser one? Is there a coarsest one? The answer to both questions is
â€œyes.â€ Indeed, the intersection of any topologies on S for which f is continuous is again a
topology on S for which f is continuous. Therefore, the intersection of all topologies on
S for which f is continuous will be the coarsest topology for which f is continuous. Letâ€™s
call it Tf and observe that it has the simple description {f âˆ’1U | U âŠ†X is open}. This shows
that the subspace topology TY on a subset Y âŠ†X is the same as Ti where i: Y â†’X is the
natural inclusion. This prompts us to adopt a better deï¬nition for the subspace topology.
Better deï¬nition
Let (X, TX) be a topological space and let Y be any subset of X. The
subspace topology on Y is the coarsest topology on Y for which the canonical inclusion
i: Y ,â†’X is continuous.
More generally, if S is any set and if f : S â†’X is an injective function, then Tf â€”the
coarsest topology on S for which f is continuousâ€”may be called the subspace topology
on S . This is a good deï¬nition even though the set S is not necessarily a subset of X.

26
Chapter 1
Why? Since f is injective, S is isomorphic as a set to its image fS âŠ†X, and the space
(S, Tf ) determined by f : S â†’X is homeomorphic to fS âŠ†X with the subspace topology
determined by the inclusion i: fS ,â†’X. (If f : S â†’X is not injective, then there is still
a coarsest topology Tf on S that makes f continuous, though we wonâ€™t refer to it as the
subspace topology.)
Deï¬nition 1.2 Suppose f : Y â†’X is a continuous injection between topological spaces.
The map f is called an embedding when the topology on Y is the same as the subspace
topology Tf induced by f.
Example 1.14 Consider the set [0, 1] with the discrete topology.
The evident map

Notice that endowing a subset Y âŠ†X with the subspace topology provides meaning to the
question, â€œIs a function Z â†’Y continuous?â€ Simply put, the subspace topology deter-
mines continuous maps to Y. The converse holds as well: continuous maps to Y determine
the topology on Y. This is yet another illustration of the philosophy that objects in a cat-
egory are determined by morphisms to and from them. Itâ€™s also the heart of the second
characterization of the subspace topology.
1.2.2

1.6
Homotopy and the Homotopy Category
Weâ€™ll close this chapter with an important application of the product topology, namely
homotopy. A homotopy from a map f : X â†’Y to a map g: X â†’Y is a continuous
function h: X Ã— [0, 1] â†’Y satisfying h(x, 0) = f x and h(x, 1) = gx. (Notice that â€œthe
topological space X Ã— [0, 1]â€ makes sense now that we have the product topology!) Two
maps f, g: X â†’Y are said to be homotopic if there is a homotopy between them, in which
case we write f â‰ƒg. Homotopy deï¬nes an equivalence relation on the maps Top(X, Y), the
equivalence classes of which are called homotopy classes of maps. We use [f] to denote
the homotopy class of f, and we use the notation [X, Y] for the set of homotopy classes
of maps. You can check that the composition of homotopic maps are homotopic, and
thus composition of homotopy classes of maps is well deï¬ned. The homotopy category of
topological spaces is deï¬ned to be the category hTop whose objects are topological spaces
and whose morphisms are homotopy classes of maps:
hTop(X, Y) := [X, Y]
Two spaces are said to be homotopic if and only if they are isomorphic in hTop. That is,
X and Y are homotopic if and only if there exist maps f : X â†’Y and g: Y â†’X so that
g f â‰ƒidX and fg â‰ƒidY. In this case we write X â‰ƒY. A homotopy invariant is a property
that is invariant under homotopy equivalence. More precisely, there is a natural functor
Top â†’hTop
with X 7â†’X and f 7â†’[f]. Functors from hTop are homotopy invariants, and functors from
the category Top that factor through the functor Top â†’hTop are called homotopy functors.
Example 1.21 The space Rn is homotopic to the one-point space, Rn â‰ƒâˆ—. To see this,
deï¬ne f : âˆ—â†’Rn by âˆ—7â†’0 and deï¬ne g: Rn â†’âˆ—in the only way possible. Then
g f = idâˆ—, and fg: Rn â†’0 is homotopic to idRn via the homotopy h: Rn Ã— [0, 1] â†’Rn

Examples and Constructions
35
given by h(x, t) = tx. Spaces such as Rn that are homotopic to a point are said to be
contractible.
Quite often, a restricted notion of homotopy applies. For example, if Î±, Î²: [0, 1] â†’X are
paths from x to y, then a homotopy of paths is deï¬ned to be h: [0, 1]Ã—[0, 1] â†’Y satisfying
h(t, 0) = Î±t, h(t, 1) = Î²t, and h(0, s) = x and h(1, s) = y for all s, t âˆˆ[0, 1]. In other words,
the homotopy ï¬xes the endpoints of the path: for all s, the path t 7â†’h(t, s) is a path from
x to y agreeing with Î± at s = 0 and with Î² at s = 1. At this point, you might wonder
why weâ€™re interested in ï¬xing the endpoints of paths throughout a homotopy of paths. The
reason is simple. Without this requirement, way too many paths may become homotopic:
you could continuously â€œwind inâ€ the endpoint of a path until it meets the initial point, then
move the initial point around, then expand the point to be another path.
Succinctly put, homotopy in topology is of great importance. Weâ€™ll return to it in much
more detail in chapter 6.

2
Connectedness and Compactness
One of the classical aims of topology is to classify topological spaces by their topological type, or in
other terms to ï¬nd a complete set of topological invariants.
â€”Samuel Eilenberg (1949)
Introduction.
In chapter 1, we discussed four main constructions of topological spaces: sub-
spaces, quotients, products, and coproducts. In this chapter, weâ€™ll see how these constructions inter-
act with three main topological properties: connectedness, Hausdorff, and compactness. That is, are
subspaces of compact spaces also compact? Is the quotient of a Hausdorff space itself Hausdorff?
Are products of connected spaces also connected? Is a union of connected spaces connected? Weâ€™ll
explore these questions and more in the pages to come.
Section 2.1 contains a survey of basic notions, theorems, and examples of connectedness. It also
includes a statement and categorical proof of the one-dimensional version of Brouwerâ€™s well-known
ï¬xed-point theorem. Section 2.2 contains the Hausdorff property, though weâ€™ll keep the discussion
brief. The Hausdorff property becomes much richer once itâ€™s combined with compactness, which is
the content of section 2.3. The same section also introduces three familiar theoremsâ€”the Bolzano-
Weierstrass theorem, the Heine-Borel theorem, and Tychonoffâ€™s theorem.
2.1
Connectedness
Weâ€™ll begin with a discussion of the main ideas about connectedness. The deï¬nitions are
collected up front and the main results follow. The proofs are mostly left as exercises, but
they can be found in most any classic text on topology, such as Willard (1970), Munkres
(2000), Kelley (1955), Lipschutz (1965).
2.1.1
Deï¬nitions, Theorems, and Examples
Deï¬nition 2.1 A topological space X is connected if and only if one of the following
equivalent conditions holds:
(i)
X cannot be expressed as the union of two disjoint nonempty open sets.
(ii)
Every continuous function f : X â†’{0, 1} is constant, where {0, 1} is equipped with
the discrete topology.

Theorems 2.3 and 2.2 illustrate common strategies in mathematics. Theorem 2.3 involves a
space decomposed into a collection of open sets. Information about each open set (theyâ€™re
connected) and information about the intersection (itâ€™s nonempty) provides information
about the whole space (itâ€™s connected). On the other hand, theorem 2.2 involves a space X
decomposed into ï¬bers over a base space. Here, information about the base space (itâ€™s con-
nected) and information about the ï¬bers (theyâ€™re connected) provides information about the
total space (itâ€™s connected). This approach to extending knowledge of parts to knowledge
of the whole appears over and over again in mathematics. Something else that commonly
appears in mathematics is giving counterexamples to help illuminate deï¬nitions.

Theorem 2.5 Path connected implies connected.
Proof. Suppose X is path connected, and let k: X â†’{0, 1} be a function. Choose any two
points in X. There exists a path connecting them. Since k must be constant on that path, it
takes the same value at these two points. Therefore k is constant.
Theorem 2.6 Connected and path connected are homotopy invariants.

be a homotopy from fg to idY.

The connectedness of I has other nice consequences. We begin with a fun result we found
in Nandakumar and Rao (2012) and Ziegler (2015).
Theorem 2.7 Every convex polygon can be partitioned into two convex polygons, each
having the same area and same perimeter.

Connectedness and Compactness
43
Proof. Let P be a convex polygon, and ï¬rst observe that ï¬nding a line that bisects the area
of P is not difï¬cult. Simply take a vertical line and consider the difference of the area on
the left and the right. As the line moves from left to right the difference goes from negative
to positive continuously and therefore must be zero at some point. Of course, there was
nothing special about a vertical line. Thereâ€™s a line in every direction which bisects P. So
start with the vertical line, and consider the difference between the perimeter on the left
and the perimeter on the right. Rotate this line in such a way that it always bisects the area
of P, and note that the difference between the perimeters switches sign as the line goes
halfway around. Therefore there exists a line that cuts P into two convex polygons, both
with equal areas and equal perimeters.

2.1.3
Constructions and Connectedness
In chapter 1, we worked through the constructions of new topological spaces from old
ones. So far in this chapter, our discussion has centered on two topological properties: con-
nectedness and path connectedness. Weâ€™ve already seen some interactions between these
properties and the constructions, but letâ€™s systematically run through the the constructions
and check whether they preserve connectedness. Quotients do, as stated in corollary 2.1.2.
Subspaces donâ€™t preserve connectedness: it doesnâ€™t take much imagination to come up with
an example. Neither do coproductsâ€”the disjoint union of two connected spaces wonâ€™t be
connectedâ€”but remember theorem 2.3 if the union is not disjoint. Products, as the next
theorem shows, do preserve connectedness.

that Ï€Î±p = pÎ± for all Î± is continuous, and moreover p is a path from a to b.

The connected components of X are open.
(iii)
The quotient space X/âˆ¼of X by its connected components is discrete.
Recall from deï¬nition 2.1 that a space is connected if and only if the only maps from it to
a two-point discrete space are constant. Letâ€™s make this a little more categorical. Observe
that for any space X there is exactly one function X â†’âˆ—. And letâ€™s think of a two-point
discrete space as the coproduct âˆ—` âˆ—. Now if X is connected, then there are precisely
two maps X â†’âˆ—` âˆ—; namely, the two constant functions: X maps to the ï¬rst point and X
maps to the second point. So the set Top(X, âˆ—` âˆ—) is the two-point set, which is canonically
isomorphic to Top(X, âˆ—) ` Top(X, âˆ—).
However, if X is not connected, then there are more than two maps Top(X, âˆ—` âˆ—). For
example, if X = [0, 1] âˆª[2, 3], then there are four functions X â†’âˆ—` âˆ—. So the set
Top(X, âˆ—` âˆ—) is not equal to Top(X, âˆ—) ` Top(X, âˆ—). These observations motivate a deï¬-
nition of connectedness that makes sense in any category that has coproducts, including
Top.
Theorem 2.11 A space X is connected if and only if the functor Top(X, âˆ’) preserves co-
products.
For more information, the categorically minded reader is encouraged to consult the entry
on connectedness at the nLab (Stacey et al., 2019).
Wrapping up this brief excursion on constructions, weâ€™ve seen that connectedness and
path-connectedness are preserved by products and quotients but are not preserved by sub-
spaces or coproducts. With that in mind, letâ€™s now turn our attention to a local version of
connectedness.

In the previous section, we discussed connectedness, which in a sense describes when

2.3

Corollary 2.15.1 Compactness is a topological property.
One way to think of compact spaces is that they are somehow smallâ€”not in terms of
cardinality but in terms of roominess. For example, if you squeeze an inï¬nite set of points

than Îµ apart. But itâ€™s easy to ï¬t an inï¬nite number of points in the real line so that theyâ€™re

point of a space X if every neighborhood of x contains a point of X âˆ–{x}.

point.

Limits of Sequences and Filters
The Axiom of Choice is obviously true, the well-ordering theorem is obviously false; and who can
tell about Zornâ€™s Lemma?

Introduction.
Chapter 2 featured various properties of topological spaces and explored their
interactions with a few categorical constructions. In this chapter weâ€™ll again discuss some topological
properties, this time with an eye toward more ï¬ne-grained ideas. As introduced early in a study of
analysis, properties of nice topological spaces X can be detected by sequences of points in X. Weâ€™ll
be interested in some of these properties and the extent to which sequences sufï¬ce to detect them.
But take note of the adjective â€œniceâ€ here. What if X is any topological space, not just a nice one?
Unfortunately, sequences are not well suited for characterizing properties in arbitrary spaces. But all
is not lost. A sequence can be replaced with a more general constructionâ€”a ï¬lterâ€”which is much
better suited for the task. In this chapter we introduce ï¬lters and highlight some of their strengths.
Our goal is to spend a little time inside of spaces to discuss ideas that may be familiar from analysis.
For this reason, this chapter contains less category theory than others. On the other hand, weâ€™ll see in
section 3.3 that ï¬lters are a bit like functors and hence like generalizations of points. This perspective
thus gives us a coarse-grained approach to investigating ï¬ne-grained ideas. Weâ€™ll go through some
of these basic ideasâ€”closure, limit points, sequences, and moreâ€”rather quickly in sections 3.1 and
3.2. Later in section 3.2 weâ€™ll see exactly why sequences donâ€™t sufï¬ce to detect certain properties
in all spaces. Weâ€™ll also discover those â€œniceâ€ spaces for which they do. Section 3.3 introduces
ï¬lters with some examples and results about them. These results include the claim that ï¬lters, unlike
sequences, do sufï¬ce to characterize certain properties. Finally, in section 3.4 weâ€™ll use ï¬lters to
share a delightfully short proof of Tychonoffâ€™s theorem.
3.1
Closure and Interior
Here are a few basic deï¬nitions, which may be familiar from analysis. Given any subset
B of a space X, two topological constructions suggest themselves. There is the closure
B which is the smallest closed set containing B, and there is the interior Bâ—¦which is the
largest open set contained in B. When B = X, we say B is dense in X. If

B
â—¦= âˆ…, we say
B is nowhere dense.

56
Chapter 3
Notice that the deï¬nition of a topology guarantees that the interior and closure exist. For
example, because a topology is closed under arbitrary unions, the interior Bâ—¦is precisely
the union of all open subsets of B. Contrast this with the ideas of a â€œlargest closed setâ€
contained in B and a â€œsmallest open setâ€ containing B, which might not exist.
A point x is called a limit point of a set B if every open set around x contains a point of
B âˆ–{x}. The closure B consists of B together with all of its limit points. A point x is called
a boundary point of B if every open set containing x contains both a point in B and a point
in the complement of B.
Limit points help to understand closures and interiors. So, taking a cue from analysis,
letâ€™s turn to a study of sequences in an attempt to characterize limit points.
3.2
Sequences
Deï¬nition 3.1 Let X be a topological space. A sequence in X is a function x: N â†’X. We
usually write xn for x(n) and may denote the sequence by {xn}. A sequence {xn} converges
to z âˆˆX if and only if for every open set U containing z, there exists an N âˆˆN so that if
n â‰¥N then xn âˆˆU. When {xn} converges to z âˆˆX weâ€™ll write {xn} â†’z. A subsequence of
a sequence x is the composition xk where k: N â†’N is an increasing injection. Weâ€™ll write
xki for xk(i) and denote the subsequence by {xki}.
Here are a few examples.
Example 3.1 Let A = {1, 2, 3} with the topology T = âˆ…, {1}, {1, 2}, A	. The constant
sequence 1, 1, 1, 1, . . . converges to 1. It also converges to 2 and to 3.
Example 3.2 Consider Z with the coï¬nite topology. For any m âˆˆZ, the constant sequence
m, m, m, . . . converges to m and only to m. Indeed if l , m, then the set R âˆ–m is an open
set around l containing no elements of the sequence.
However, the sequence {n} = 1, 2, 3, 4, . . . converges to m for every m âˆˆZ. To see this,
let m be any integer, and let U be a neighborhood of m. Since Z âˆ–U is ï¬nite, there can
only be ï¬nitely many natural numbers in Z âˆ–U. Let N be larger than the greatest natural
number in Z âˆ–U. Then n âˆˆU whenever n â‰¥N, proving that {n} â†’m.
Example 3.3 Consider R with the usual topology. If {xn} â†’x, then {xn} does not converge
to any number y , x. To prove it, note we can always ï¬nd disjoint open sets U and V with
x âˆˆU and y âˆˆV. (We can be explicit if necessary: U = (x âˆ’c, x + c) and V = (y âˆ’c, y + c)
where c =
1
2|x âˆ’y|.) Then there is a number N so that xn âˆˆU for all n â‰¥N. Since
U âˆ©V = âˆ…, the set V cannot contain any xn for n â‰¥N, and hence {xn} does not converge
to y.
As weâ€™ll see below, sequences can be used to detect certain properties of spaces, subsets

Theorem 3.1 A space X is T1 if and only if for any x âˆˆX, the constant sequence x, x, x, . . .
converges to x and only to x.
Proof. Suppose X is T1 and x âˆˆX. Itâ€™s clear that x, x, x, . . . â†’x. Let y , x. Then there
exists an open set U with y âˆˆU and x < U. Therefore, x, x, x, . . . cannot converge to y.
For the converse, suppose X is not T1. Then there exist two distinct points x and y for

Theorem 3.2 If X is Hausdorff, then sequences in X have at most one limit.
Proof. Let X be Hausdorff and suppose {xn} is a sequence such that {xn} â†’x and {xn} â†’y.

and xM âˆˆV, contradicting the fact that U and V are disjoint.

The reason sequences characterize separation, closure, and continuity in ï¬rst countable

we want the previous three if-and-only-if theorems to hold in a wider context, then weâ€™ll

Exn := {A âŠ†X | there exists an N so that xn âˆˆA for all n â‰¥N}

Understanding convergence, therefore, amounts to understanding the set Exn. This immedi-

3.3
Filters and Convergence
A ï¬lter is like an algebraic road map with (perhaps rough) directions to points or places in
a space. More precisely, it is a certain subset of a poset (a partially ordered set). In this
chapter, the poset we focus on is the powerset of a set X. That is, we consider ï¬lters of
subsets of a given set. Here is the deï¬nition.
Deï¬nition 3.4 A ï¬lter on a set X is a collection F âŠ†2X that is
(i)
downward directed: A, B âˆˆF implies there exists C âˆˆF such that C âŠ†A âˆ©B
(ii)
nonempty: F , âˆ…
(iii)
upward closed: A âˆˆF and A âŠ†B implies B âˆˆF
An additional property is often useful:
(iv)
proper: there exists A âŠ†X such that A < F
So a ï¬lter on X is a downward directed, nonempty, upward closed subset of the powerset
2X. A couple of comments are in order. Being downward directed and upward closed

as the requirement that âˆ…< F . For example, 2X is itself a ï¬lter and is well named the
improper ï¬lter on X. A set that is only downward directed and nonempty is called a
ï¬lterbase. Any ï¬lterbase generates a ï¬lter; just take the upward closure of the base.

An ultraï¬lter is simply a ï¬lter that is maximal. The terms are synonymous.
Deï¬nition 3.7 A proper ï¬lter on a set is an ultraï¬lter if and only if it is not properly

This deï¬nition is second order since it deals with a quantiï¬cation over subsets of a set. In

Proposition 3.1 A ï¬lter U on a set X is an ultraï¬lter if and only if for every subset A âŠ†X

Theorem 3.11 A ï¬lter on X is maximal if and only if it is prime.
Proof. Suppose F is an ultraï¬lter on X and fails to be prime. Then there are A, B âŠ†X
such that AâˆªB âˆˆF , but neither A nor B are in F . The latter holds if and only if there exist
sets Aâ€², Bâ€² âˆˆF with A âˆ©Aâ€² = âˆ…= B âˆ©Bâ€². This implies the intersection (A âˆªB) âˆ©(Aâ€² âˆªBâ€²)
is empty, which is true if and only if A âˆªB < F , a clear contradiction.
Now suppose F is prime but not a maximal. Then F is properly contained in a proper

otherwise X âˆ–A âˆˆG and if G contains both A and X âˆ–A, then G would not be proper. But
A âˆª(X âˆ–A) = X âˆˆF , contradicting the hypothesis that F is prime.
Below, weâ€™ll use this theorem to give a succinct characterization of compact spaces. But
despite the theorem, it will be good to keep a distinction between prime and maximal in our
minds. There are several reasons why. First, as alluded to above, prime and maximal are
not equivalent in more general settings. Keeping the two distinct in our minds strengthens
our intuition. Second, a priori prime ï¬lters are difï¬cult to construct. But as weâ€™ll soon see,
we can always consider a maximal extension of a proper ï¬lter and then use the fact that
ultraï¬lters are prime. Finally, the theorems proven below are naturally phrased in terms of
prime ï¬lters because images commute with unions and therefore prime ï¬lters pushforward.
Keeping the distinction thus makes proving theorems easier: if you need a prime ï¬lter, just
construct one by extending a proper ï¬lter. If your construction involves pushing a ï¬lter
forward, then prime ï¬lters are your friend. Now, en route to compactness letâ€™s prove our
claim that every proper ï¬lter can be extended to a maximal one. Weâ€™ll call on Zornâ€™s
lemma, whose statement we recount here.
Zornâ€™s Lemma
If every chain in a nonempty poset P has an upper bound, then P has a
maximal element.
The Ultraï¬lter Lemma
Every proper ï¬lter is contained in an ultraï¬lter.
Proof. Any set of ï¬lters {FÎ±}Î±âˆˆA is bounded above by the ï¬lter generated by ï¬nite inter-
sections of elements of the {FÎ±}. When the set is a chain of proper ï¬lters, this upper bound
is itself proper. Given a proper ï¬lter F , chains of proper ï¬lters containing F thus have
proper upper bounds. By Zornâ€™s Lemma, there is a maximal ï¬lter containing F .
Corollary 3.11.1 Any inï¬nite set has a non-principal ultraï¬lter.
Proof. Consider the FrÂ´echet ï¬lter F := {A âŠ†X | X âˆ–A is ï¬nite} and appeal to the Ultra-

Youâ€™ll recognize this theorem as a generalization of the Bolzano-Weierstrass theorem in-

Corollary 3.12.1 A space X is compact Hausdorff if and only if every prime ï¬lter has
exactly one limit point.
This characterization of compact Hausdorff spaces is the beginning of a long categorical
tale. Sharing the full story would take us too far off course, so instead weâ€™ll tell an abridged
version. It starts with the fact that ultraï¬lters deï¬ne a functor from the category Set to itself,
a consequence of this next theorem.
Theorem 3.13 Let U be an ultraï¬lter on X and let f : X â†’Y. The pushforward fâˆ—U is
an ultraï¬lter on Y.
Proof. Exercise.
Since the pushforward of an ultraï¬lter is an ultraï¬lter, the assignment Î²: Set â†’Set that
sends a set X to Î²X, the set of ultraï¬lters on X, deï¬nes a functor. For a morphism f : X â†’Y

Whatâ€™s the ultraï¬lter monad? And what does it mean to be an algebra for one? We wonâ€™t
get into the details, but we will try to give you an idea about what the statement means.
Principal ï¬lters play a principal role: since the pushforward of a principal ï¬lter is principal,
they assemble into a natural transformation Î· : idSet â†’Î² deï¬ned by
Î·X(x) = Px
where Px is the principal ï¬lter at x âˆˆX. There is another natural transformation Âµ: Î²â—¦Î² â†’

multiplication, and the natural transformation Î· behaves like a unit for this multiplication.
The triple (Î², Î·, Âµ) deï¬nes something called a monad. (It should remind you of a monoid,
which also consists of three things: a set X, an associative multiplication map m: X Ã— X â†’
X, and an element u: âˆ—â†’X behaving as a unit for m.) Once you have a monad, you
can deï¬ne something called an algebra for that monad, and the algebras for a monad form
a category. And this category, one can show, is equivalent to the category of compact
Hausdorff spaces. Behind the curtain of this categorical connection between ultraï¬lters and
compact Hausdorff spaces is the rich theory of adjunctions. For this reason, the story will
naturally resurface later in this book: itâ€™s closely related to the discussion of the Stone- Ë‡Cech
compactiï¬cation in chapter 5. For an introduction to monads, see Riehl (2016), and for a
fuller account of the ultraï¬lter monad tale, see Manes (1969) and the article on compacta
at the nLab (Stacey et al., 2019). The ambitious reader may further enjoy Leinster (2013).
After this leisurely stroll through ultraï¬lters, prime ï¬lters, Bolzano-Weierstrass, and

3.4.2
A Proof of Tychonoffâ€™s Theorem
There is a conservation of difï¬culty in mathematics. One theorem may have many proofs,
and more sophisticated tools will give more elegant proofs. Historically, the difï¬culties
in Tychonoffâ€™s theorem were in ï¬nding the correct deï¬nition of the product topology and
in characterizing compactness. By using ultraï¬lters to characterize compactness, we are
using a sophisticated theoretical tool. The proof we share is correspondingly elegant (Cher-
noff, 1992).
If sequences had been sufï¬cient for discussions of convergence, then we could use a
ready-made argument. Just use continuity of projection maps from the product to push a
sequence forward. In each factor, pass to a subsequence, which converges by Bolzano-

Limits of Sequences and Filters
69
Weierstrass. Then in the product, form a subsequence by taking the indices common to
all the convergent subsequences in the projections, and conclude this sequence converges.
Hereâ€™s the thrill: by replacing sequences with ï¬lters and doing the work needed to develop
some understanding of the theory, this ready-made argument becomes a genuine proof.

3.4.3
A Little Set Theory
In any imaginable proof that the Tychonoff theorem implies the axiom of choice, one
begins with an arbitrary collection of sets and then creates a collection of compact topo-
logical spaces. The compactness of the product leads to the existence of a choice function.
In 1950, Kelley proved that the Tychonoff theorem implies the axiom of choice (Kelley,
1950) using augmented coï¬nite topologies. Here, we give an easier proof. First, we recall
the axiom of choice.

Categorical Limits and Colimits

Introduction.
Categorical limits and colimits are one of theâ€”and in some sense the mostâ€”
efï¬cient way to build a new mathematical object from old objects. The constructions introduced
in chapter 1â€”subspaces, quotients, products, and coproductsâ€”are examples in Top, though the
discussion can occur in any category. In fact, there are a number of other important constructionsâ€”
pushouts, pullbacks, direct limits, and so onâ€”so itâ€™s valuable to learn the general notion.
In practice, limits are typically built by picking a subcollection according to some constraint,
whereas colimits are typically built by â€œgluingâ€ objects together. More formally, the deï¬ning prop-
erty of a limit is characterized by morphisms whose domain is the limit. The deï¬ning property of a
colimit is characterized by morphisms whose codomain is the colimit. Because of their generality,
limits and colimits appear all across the mathematical landscape. A direct sum of abelian groups,
the least upper bound of a poset, and a CW complex are all examples of limits or colimits, and weâ€™ll
see more examples in the pages to come. Section 4.1 opens the chapter by answering the antici-
pated question, â€œA (co)limit of what?â€ The remaining two sections contain the formal deï¬nition of
(co)limits followed by a showcase of examples.
4.1

(co)limit of a diagram. In what follows, it will be helpful to view a diagram as a functor.
More speciï¬cally, a diagram in a category is a functor from the shape of the diagram to the
category. For example, a commutative diagram like this

4.3
Examples
Depending on the shape of the indexing category, the (co)limit of a diagram may be given a
familiar name: intersection, union, Cartesian product, kernel, direct sum, quotient, ï¬bered
product, and so on. The following examples illustrate this idea. In each case, recall that
the data of a (co)limit is an object together with maps to or from that object, satisfying a
universal property.
4.3.1
Terminal and Initial Objects
If the indexing category D is emptyâ€”no objects and no morphismsâ€”then a functor D â†’C
is an empty diagram. The limit of an empty diagram is called a terminal object. It is an
object T in C such that for every object X in C there is a unique morphism X â†’T. In
other words, all objects in the category terminate at T. In Set the terminal object is the
one-point set; in Top itâ€™s the one-point space; in Grp itâ€™s the trivial group; in FVect, itâ€™s
the zero vector space; in a poset, its the greatest element, if it exists. This highlights an
important point: not every category has a terminal object. For example, R with the usual
ordering is a poset without a greatest elementâ€”it is a category without a terminal object.
Dually, the colimit of an empty diagram is called an initial object. It is an object I in C
such that for every object X in C there is a unique morphism I â†’X. In other words, all
objects in the category initialize from I. In Set the initial object is the empty set; in Top

poset, itâ€™s the least element, if it exists. Again, not every category has an initial object.
Many times, when one is interested in the limit of a diagram, the colimit of the diagram
will be trivial, or vice versa. For example, the colimit of a diagram that has a terminal

4.3.2
Products and Coproducts
If the indexing category D has no nonidentity morphismsâ€”that is, if D is a discrete cat-
egoryâ€”then a diagram D â†’C is just a collection of objects parametrized by D. In this
case, the limit of the diagram is called the product and the colimit is called the coproduct.
When C = Set, you can verify the universal properties of the product and coproduct given
in chapter 0 and show that they are indeed the limit and colimit of discrete diagrams. When
the category is Top, the limit is the product of the spaces in the diagram, equipped with the
product topology, together with projection maps, down to each of the factors. Likewise, the
coproduct is the disjoint union of spaces, equipped with the coproduct topology, together
with inclusion maps from each space.
Quite often, one is only interested in a subset (or subspace) of the product. For instance,
given sets or spaces X and Y, itâ€™s often interesting to consider only those pairs (x, y) âˆˆ

may be interested in identifying parts of sets (or spaces) rather than considering their full

After reading through the examples in this chapter, you might suspect that a limit is
always, in some sense, either a product or a construction obtained from a product. This
suspicion is indeed correct and provides a prescription for constructing limits in general. In
fact, it is a theorem: if a category has all products and all equalizers, then it has all limits.
Likewise, the feeling that a colimit may be regarded as either a coproduct or a quotient of

86
Chapter 4
a coproduct is also a theorem: if a category has all coproducts and all coequalizers, then it
has all colimits. Weâ€™ll close with these results in the next section.
4.4
Completeness and Cocompleteness
A category is called complete if it contains the limits of small diagrams and is called co-
complete if it contains the colimits of all small diagrams.1 The categories Set and Top are
both complete and cocomplete. In Set, one can construct the colimit of any diagram by
taking the disjoint union of every set in the diagram and then quotienting by the relations
required for the diagram to map into the resulting set. In Top, this set gets the quotient
topology of the disjoint union. This topology is the ï¬nest topology for which all the maps
involved in the map from the diagram are continuous.
Dually, to construct the limit of any diagram of sets, ï¬rst take the product of all the sets
that appear in the diagram. The product then maps to all objects in the diagram. The limit
of the diagram is simply the subset of the product so that the projection maps to the objects
assemble to be a map to the diagram. In Top, this set gets the subspace topology of the
product. This topology is the coarsest topology for which all the maps involved in the map
to the diagram are continuous.
Seeing how to deï¬ne an arbitrary colimit of sets as a quotient of the disjoint union or
how to deï¬ne an arbitrary limit as a subspace of the product gives the idea of how to prove
the following theorem: small (co)limits are all â€œgenerated byâ€ a setâ€™s worth of (co)products
and (co)equalizers.
Theorem 4.1 If a category has products and equalizers, then it is complete. If it has
coproducts and coequalizers, then it is cocomplete.
Proof. Hereâ€™s how to construct the colimit of a diagram in a category with coproducts and

diagram that have morphisms XÎ± â†’XÎ² from them (there may be multiple copies of XÎ±s)

each):

Adjunctions and the Compact-Open Topology
Birds ï¬‚y high in the air and survey broad vistas of mathematics out to the far horizon. They delight
in concepts that unify our thinking and bring together diverse problems from different parts of the
landscape. Frogs live in the mud below and see only the ï¬‚owers that grow nearby. They delight in
the details of particular objects, and they solve problems one at a time.
â€”Freeman Dyson (2009)
Introduction.
Early in chapter 0 it was noted that category theory is an appropriate setting in
which to discuss the concept of â€œsamenessâ€ between mathematical objects. This concept is captured
by an isomorphism: a morphism from one object to another that is both left and right invertible.
The discussion becomes especially interesting when those objects are categories. Two categories are
isomorphic if there exists a pair of functorsâ€”one in each directionâ€”whose compositions equal the
identities. But equality is a lot of ask for! Isomorphisms of categories are too strict to be of much
use. Relaxing the situation yields something better: categories C and D are equivalent if there exists
a pair of functors L: C
D: R and natural isomorphisms idC â†’RL and LR â†’idD.
Relaxing this a step further yields another gem of category theory: adjunctions. A pair of functors
L: C
D: R forms an adjunction, and L and R are called adjoint functors, if there are natural
transformations (not necessarily isomorphisms) Î·: idC â†’RL and Ïµ : LR â†’idD that, in addition,
interact compatibly in a sense that can be made precise. Here the categories may not be equivalent,
but donâ€™t think that adjunctions are mere second (or third) best. Quite often, relaxing a notion of
equivalence results in a trove of rich mathematics. That is indeed the case here.
In this chapter, then, we introduce adjoint functors and use them to highlight several construc-
tions in topology. Weâ€™ll present the formal deï¬nition in section 5.1 and give some examplesâ€”free
constructions in algebra, a forgetful functor from Top, and the Stone- Ë‡Cech compactiï¬cationâ€”in sec-
tions 5.2, 5.3, and 5.5, respectively. Then weâ€™ll use a particularly nice adjunctionâ€”the product-hom
adjunctionâ€”as motivation for putting an appropriate topology on function spaces. In section 5.6,
weâ€™ll take an in-depth look at this topology, called the compact-open topology. Quite a few pages
are devoted to this endeavor and some of the difï¬culties involved. Finally, section 5.7 closes with
a discussion on the category of compactly generated weakly Hausdorff spacesâ€”a â€œconvenientâ€ cat-
egory of topological spaces. So in the pages to come, weâ€™ll be both birds and frogs. A categorical
point of view oftentimes highlights and elevates the important properties that characterize an object
or construction but fails to establish that such objects exist. Existence can require getting down in
the mud.

92
Chapter 5
5.1
Adjunctions
As mentioned above, an adjunction consists of a pair of functors L and R and a pair of
natural transformations Î· and Ïµ that interact in a certain way. But there is an equivalent
deï¬nition, which is simpler to digest in a ï¬rst introduction. Weâ€™ll present this deï¬nition
now and jump right into an example. The alternate deï¬nition will be given shortly after.

This explains why the constructions of products and coproducts, subspaces and quotients,

5.4
Adjoint Functor Theorems
What about a converse to theorem 5.1? Let R : D â†’C be any functor. Under what
conditions will R have a left adjoint? Clearly, R must be continuous. Is the continuity of R
sufï¬cient? Not quite. Hereâ€™s a nice way to think about it. For each object X âˆˆC, look at the
category RX whose objects consist of an object Y in D together with a morphism X â†’RY.
A morphism between f : X â†’RY and f â€² : X â†’RYâ€² is a morphism g: Y â†’Yâ€² such that
(Rg)f = f â€². As in remark 5.1, given a functor L: C â†’D, an object LX âˆˆD satisfying
D(LX, Y)  C(X, RY) for all Y is an initial object in RX. If there is an initial object LX
in RX for every object X, then they assemble functorially into a left adjoint L: C â†’D.
In any category, an initial object is the limit of the identity functor. So, R will have a left
adjoint if and only if the identity functor on the category RX has a limit for all objects
X. If the category D is complete, then the functor R being continuous implies that RX is

98
Chapter 5
complete. However, even if RX is complete, the identity functor on RX is usually not a
small diagram. So, there are a suite of theorems known as adjoint functor theorems that
assume the functor R is continuous and the category D is complete and that add some kind
of hypothesis allowing one to use the fact that RX has limits of all small diagrams to prove
that the identity functor on RX has a limit. We donâ€™t use any adjoint functor theorems in
this book, but itâ€™s good to know they exist. Letâ€™s state one precisely with the Solution Set
Condition (Mac Lane, 2013; Freyd, 1969) hypothesis.
The Solution Set Condition
A functor R: D â†’C satisï¬es the Solution Set Condition
if and only if for every object X in C, there exists a set of objects {Yi} in D and a set of
morphisms
S = {fi : X â†’RYi}
so that any f : X â†’RY factors through some fi âˆˆS along a morphism Yi â†’Y in D.
The Adjoint Functor Theorem
Suppose D is complete and that R: D â†’C is a continu-
ous functor satisfying the Solution Set Condition. Then R has a left adjoint L: C â†’D.
For details beyond what weâ€™ve already said, see the classic reference by Mac Lane (2013)
or section 4.6 of the excellent book by Riehl (2016), and for an enlightening treatment of
adjunctions including applications, see Spivak (2014). Before moving on, we should say
that there are adjoint functor theorems for the existence of right adjoints as well. They
suppose that L: C â†’D is a cocontinuous functor from a cocomplete category C, with
some â€œcoâ€ version of the Solution Set Condition.
Our discussion of adjoint functor theorems arose from an observation about the forget-
ful functor on Top and its adjoints. Another notable adjunction in topology arises in a
discussion on compactiï¬cations.
5.5

Deï¬nition 5.3 A compactiï¬cation of a topological space is an embedding of the space as

i: X â†’Y with X  iX âŠ†Y and X = Y. Note that only Hausdorff spaces have compactiï¬-

5.5.1
The One-Point Compactiï¬cation
If a compactiï¬cation Y of a space X is obtained by adding a single point to X, then X ,â†’Y
is called a one-point compactiï¬cationâ€”also sometimes called the Alexandroff one-point

Adjunctions and the Compact-Open Topology
99
compactiï¬cation. A space X has one-point compactiï¬cation if and only if X is Hausdorff
and locally compact. If a space has a one-point compactiï¬cation, then itâ€™s unique.
To see this, suppose X ,â†’Xâˆ—is a compactiï¬cation and Xâˆ—âˆ–X = {p}. The open neighbor-
hoods of p are precisely the complements of compact subsets of X: the complement of an
open set containing p is a closed subset of a compact space and so is compact. Conversely
if K is a compact subset of X âŠ‚Xâˆ—, then it is closed, so its complement in Xâˆ—is an open
set containing p. Then, because points of X can be separated by open sets from the point
p âˆˆXâˆ—âˆ–X, thereâ€™s a neighborhood of every point of X contained in a compact set, so X is
locally compact. The fact that X is a dense subset of Xâˆ—implies {p} is not open, meaning
that X is not already compact.
Conversely, beginning with any space X, one constructs a new space by adding a point p
and deï¬ning the open neighborhoods of p to be complements of compact sets in X. If X is
locally compact and Hausdorff and not compact, the result is a topology on Xâˆ—:= X âˆª{p}
that is compact and Hausdorff having X as a dense subset.

5.6
The Exponential Topology
Let X and Y be spaces. Consider the general problem of equipping the set of continuous
functions Top(X, Y) with a topology making it a space of maps, or a mapping space. For
the record, the product topology is usually not an appropriate topology for Top(X, Y) since
it treats the space X only as an index setâ€”it doesnâ€™t use the topology of X except to identify
the continuous functions within the set of all functions X â†’Y. But what properties should
a topology on Top(X, Y) have? We take as guidance the following desired property.
Desired Property
For a ï¬xed space X, the functors
X Ã— âˆ’: Top â†’Top
and
Top(X, âˆ’): Top â†’Top
should form an adjoint pair. That is, for all spaces Y and Z, we should have an isomorphism
of sets Top(X Ã— Z, Y)  Top(Z, Top(X, Y)).
Letâ€™s begin to analyze this property. First think of three ï¬xed spaces X, Y, and Z. One can
obtain a function X â†’Y by starting with a function g: X Ã— Z â†’Y of two variables and
by ï¬xing one of the variables z âˆˆZ, resulting in g(âˆ’, z): X â†’Y. Weâ€™d like a topology on

Chapter 5
Top(X, Y) to have the property that, if the function g of two variables is continuous, then
the assignment z 7â†’g(âˆ’, z) will deï¬ne a continuous map Z â†’Top(X, Y). Going the other
way, if we have a continuous map Z â†’Top(X, Y), then we should be able to assemble the
family of continuous maps from X to Y that are continuously parametrized by the space Z
into a single continuous map X Ã— Z â†’Y of two variables.
Now, letâ€™s look more closely at the desired property. Let g: X Ã— Z â†’Y be continuous.
Denote the adjunct by Ë†g: Z â†’Top(X, Y). For Ë†g to be continuous, the topology on Top(X, Y)
should be rather coarse. However, if the topology on Top(X, Y) is too coarse (think of
the indiscrete topology), then the set Top(Z, Top(X, Y)) will contain too many continuous
functionsâ€”it will contain functions that are not the adjunct of any continuous map g: X Ã—
Z â†’Y. Itâ€™s a balancing act that turns out neatly; if there exists a topology on Top(X, Y) so
that for any space Z, the correspondence g 7â†’Ë†g deï¬nes a bijection of sets
Top(X Ã— Z, Y)  Top(Z, Top(X, Y))
then that topology is unique (Arens and Dugundji, 1951; EscardÂ´o and Heckmann, 2002).
Letâ€™s call it the exponential topology on Top(X, Y).
This balancing act is reminiscent of the constructions of new spaces from old in chapter
1. Thatâ€™s what weâ€™re doing here as well: given two spaces X and Y, we want to make a
new topological space from the set of continuous maps from X to Y. In chapter 1, we had
guidance from universal properties characterizing similar constructions in Set. Here, the
product-hom adjunction in Set provides our categorical guidance. Letâ€™s go through some
of the details.
Letâ€™s call a topology on Top(X, Y) splitting if the continuity of g: Z Ã— X â†’Y implies
the continuity of Ë†g: Z â†’Top(X, Y). Letâ€™s call a topology on Top(X, Y) conjoining if the
continuity of Ë†g: Z â†’Top(X, Y) implies the continuity of g: Z Ã— X â†’Y. Then, to repeat
the previous remarks using this terminology (Render, 1993), a topology on Top(X, Y) must
be rather coarse to be splitting and must be rather ï¬ne to be conjoining. A topology on
Top(X, Y) is exponential if and only if it is both splitting and conjoining.
Now keep in mind two things. First, the evaluation map is the counit of the product-hom
adjunction in Set. Second, the adjunct of the evaluation map is the identity. Together, these
give a very nice characterization of conjoining topologies.
Lemma 5.1 A topology on Top(X, Y) is conjoining if and only if the evaluation map
eval: X Ã— Top(X, Y) â†’Y is continuous.
Proof. Assume we have a topology on Top(X, Y) for which the evaluation map is contin-
uous. Consider a continuous map Ë†g: Z â†’Top(X, Y), and look at the following diagram

Lemma 5.2 Every splitting topology on Top(X, Y) is coarser than every conjoining topol-

Proof. Let T , T â€² be topologies on Top(X, Y). If T â€² is conjoining, then the evaluation map

map is the identity (Top(X, Y), T â€²) â†’(Top(X, Y), T ), we conclude that T âŠ†T â€².
The balancing act follows directly from Lemma 5.2.
Theorem 5.3 If there exists an exponential topology on Top(X, Y), then it is unique.
Proof. Suppose T and T â€² are exponential topologies on Top(X, Y). Since T is splitting
and T â€² is conjoining, we have T âŠ†T â€². And vice versa: we have T â€² âŠ†T since T â€² is
splitting and T is conjoining.
The catch, as you might have guessed, is that there might not exist an exponential topology
on Top(X, Y)â€”there may be a gap between the splitting topologies and the conjoining
topologies on Top(X, Y). So at this point, you might wonder about taking an â€œadjoint
functor theoremâ€ approach to ï¬nding a right adjoint to the functor X Ã— âˆ’: Top â†’Top. To
do so, we would have to explore the extent to which X Ã— âˆ’preserves colimits. Whether
X Ã—âˆ’preserves colimits depends on X. The spaces X for which the functor X Ã—âˆ’preserves
colimits were characterized in 1970 as those spaces that are core-compact (Day and Kelly,
1970). Weâ€™ll skip the deï¬nition of core-compact here, except to say that a Hausdorff space
is core-compact if and only if it is locally compact. It follows that for a locally compact
Hausdorff space X, there does exist an exponential topology on Top(X, Y) for any space
Y. Even better, this exponential topology, when X is locally compact Hausdorff, coincides
with what is classically called the compact-open topology (Fox, 1945). So, at least when X
is locally compact and Hausdorff, we can get started thinking about the desired categorical
properties of mapping spaces using ideas in classical topology. This is what we do in the
next few sections.
Before going on to the compact-open topology, we should give a general categorical
deï¬nition. In any category C that has ï¬nite products, one can ask if for all X, Y âˆˆC the set
C(X, Y) can be considered as an object in C and if so, whether it supports a product-hom
adjunction X Ã— âˆ’: C
C: C(X, âˆ’). If the answer is yes, then the category is referred to

104
Chapter 5
as Cartesian closed. The fact that exponential topologies do not always exist implies that
the category Top is not Cartesian closed. One might try to ï¬nd a â€œconvenientâ€ category
of topological spaces in which the product-hom adjunction holds that is also rich enough
to contain the spaces we care about (Brown, 2006; Isbell, 1975; Steenrod, 1967; Stacey
et al., 2019). One might guess that locally compact Hausdorff spaces are such a category.
But no. Nevermind that we might want some non-Hausdorff spaces. If X and Y are both
locally compact and Hausdorff, then Top(X, Y) may not be. In the ï¬nal section 5.7 of this
chapter, we discuss the search for a convenient Cartesian closed category of topological
spaces. That discussion will involve a shift in perspective that, once again, is illuminated
with adjunctions.
5.6.1
The Compact-Open Topology
Letâ€™s now deï¬ne the compact-open topology, and try to give you a feel for it.
Deï¬nition 5.4 Let X and Y be topological spaces. For each compact set K âŠ†X and each
open set U âŠ†Y, deï¬ne S (K, U) := {f âˆˆTop(X, Y) | f K âŠ†U}. The sets S (K, U) form a
subbasis for a topology on Top(X, Y) called the compact-open topology.
Notice that a subbasis for the product topology on Top(X, Y) consists of sets
S (F, U) = {(f : X â†’Y) | f F âŠ†U}
where F âŠ†X is ï¬nite and U âŠ†Y is open. That is, the product topology is what one
might call the â€œï¬nite-openâ€ topology. In the case when X has the discrete topology, all
functions X â†’Y are continuous and the compact-open topology on Top(X, Y) coincides
with the product topology on Top(X, Y). More generally, every ï¬nite set is compact, so the
compact-open topology is ï¬ner than the product-topology. As a consequence, fewer ï¬lters
converge in the compact-open topology than in the product topology.
In fact, a closer look at convergence can give you a good feel for the difference between
the product topology and the compact-open topology. Letâ€™s start by looking at sequences.
A sequence of functions {fn : [0, 1] â†’[0, 1]}nâˆˆN converges to a limiting function f in
the product topology if and only if the sequence converges pointwise. On the other hand, a
sequence of functions {fn : [0, 1] â†’[0, 1]}nâˆˆN converges to f in the compact-open topology
if and only if the sequence converges uniformly. To see this, consider a more general
situation. Suppose that X is compact and Y is a metric space. Then Top(X, Y) becomes a
metric space with the metric deï¬ned by

5.6.2
The Theorems of Ascoli and Arzela
Itâ€™s not difï¬cult to decide when a family of functions is compact using the product topology.
Theorem 5.8 If X is any space and Y is Hausdorff, then a subset A âŠ†Top(X, Y) has
compact closure in the product topology if and only if for each x âˆˆX, the set Ax = {f x âˆˆ
Y | f âˆˆA} has compact closure in Y.
Proof. This was exercise 2.19 at the end of chapter 2.
If we can identify families of functions for which the product topology and the compact-
open topology coincide, then we have necessary and sufï¬cient conditions for such families
to be compact in the compact-open topology. The following deï¬nition provides a common
way to make such an identiï¬cation.
Deï¬nition 5.5 Let X be a topological space and (Y, d) be a metric space. A family A âŠ†
Top(X, Y) is called equicontinuous at x âˆˆX if and only if for every Îµ > 0, there exists an
open neighborhood U of x so that for every u âˆˆU and for every f âˆˆA, d(f x, fu) < Îµ. If
F is equicontinuous for every x âˆˆX, the family A is simply called equicontinuous.
So a family of functions is equicontinuous if, within a neighborhood, one can bound the
variation of every function in the family by a single epsilon. For this section, the most
important facts about equicontinuous families are that the compact-open topology agrees
with the product topology on them and that their closures are also equicontinuous. We
leave the proofs of these two facts as exercises for you to solve.
Lemma 5.5 Let X be a topological space and (Y, d) be a metric space. If A âŠ†Top(X, Y)
is an equicontinuous family, then the subspace topology on A of Top(X, Y) with compact-
open topology is the same as the subspace topology on A of Top(X, Y) with the product
topology.
Lemma 5.6 If A âŠ†Top(X, Y) is equicontinuous, then the closure of A in Top(X, Y) using
the product topology is also equicontinuous.
Putting these ideas together gives us the famous theorems of Ascoli and Arzela.
Ascoliâ€™s Theorem
Let X be locally compact Hausdorff, and let (Y, d) be a metric space.
A family F âŠ†Top(X, Y) has compact closure if and only if F is equicontinuous and for
every x âˆˆX, the set Fx := {f x | f âˆˆF } has compact closure.
Arzelaâ€™s Theorem
Let X be compact, (Y, d) be a metric space and {fn} be a sequence
of functions in Top(X, Y). If {fn} is equicontinuous and if for each x âˆˆX the set {fnx} is
bounded, then {fn} has a subsequence that converges uniformly.
